2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAn expression is a combination of values, function calls and operations that, when evaluated, produces an output value. An expression may or may not have side effects.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhich of the following are expressions?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ef(x)*5\n-- Yes!\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eif (foo)\ncout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; \"bar\";\n-- No\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ea = 5\n-- In some languages, yes. Like in C++ we can do b = a = 5, so a = 5 is an expression that has a side effect and produces a result\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ea \u0026amp;\u0026amp; (b || c)\n-- Yes!\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eif a \u0026gt; 5 then a\u003cem\u003e3 else a\u003c/em\u003e4\n-- Yes - in functional languages (or languages with functional features) this if code is an expression, which produces a result of either a\u003cem\u003e3 or a\u003c/em\u003e4\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch2 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eExpression Evaluation Order\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-expression-evaluation-order\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Expression Evaluation Order\" href=\"#expression-evaluation-order\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eConsider the following C++ code:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"int c = 1, d = 2;\n\nint f() {\n  ++c;\n  return 0;\n}\n\nint main() {\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; c - d + f() ;\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e c = \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e1\u003c/span\u003e, d = \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e2\u003c/span\u003e;\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003ef\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  ++c;\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003ereturn\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e0\u003c/span\u003e;\n}\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; c - d + \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ef\u003c/span\u003e() ;\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eOr this code:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"int c = 0; \n\nint f() {\n  ++c;\n  return c;\n}\n\nvoid h(int a, int b)\n  { cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; a \u0026lt;\u0026lt; \u0026quot; \u0026quot;\u0026lt;\u0026lt; b; }\n\nint main() {\n  h(c, f());\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e c = \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e0\u003c/span\u003e; \n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003ef\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  ++c;\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003ereturn\u003c/span\u003e c;\n}\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003evoid\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eh\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e a, \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e b)\n  { cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; a \u0026lt;\u0026lt; \u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-pds\"\u003e\"\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-pds\"\u003e\"\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u0026lt;\u0026lt; b; }\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003eh\u003c/span\u003e(c, \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ef\u003c/span\u003e());\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eChallenge: What will these C++ programs print?\u003cbr\u003e\nThe first program produces either -1 or 0\nThe second program produces either 0 1 or 1 1\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhy? C++ doesn't specify what order the terms of an arithmetic expression must be evaluated! Nor does it specify what order parameters must be evaluated! Some languages mandate a left-to-right eval order (C#, Java, JavaScript, Swift), while others don't (C++, Rust, OCaml).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo in general, if your expressions are calling functions, those functions shouldn't have side effects that could impact the execution of the other functions called in the expression. Otherwise you'll get unpredictable behavior depending on the compiler and potentially each time you compile the code it could do a different thing!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhy avoid specifiying ane explicit evaluation order? The reason that an ordering is not specified is to give the compiler latitude to optimize the code.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch2 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eAssociativity\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-associativity\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Associativity\" href=\"#associativity\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eRegardless what order each of the terms are evaluated... Most languages use left-to-right associativity when evaluating mathematical expressions. Which is the way we all learned to do it in school.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo an expression like:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; c - d + f() ;\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003ecout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; c - d + f() ;\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWould be evaluated as:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; (c - d) + f() ;\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003ecout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; (c - d) + f() ;\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eInstead of:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; c - (d + f());\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003ecout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; c - (d + f());\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThere are some notable exceptions, like evaluating exponentiation (e.g., 2^3^2 == 512, since we often evaluate exponentiation from right-to-left).  Assignment is also right-associative, so we can do something like this:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"  a = b = c = 5;  \n  // equivalent to:\n  a  = (b = (c = 5));   // so c = 5 runs first, then b = c runs, then a = b runs\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e  a = b = c = \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e5\u003c/span\u003e;  \n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e//\u003c/span\u003e equivalent to:\u003c/span\u003e\n  a  = (b = (c = \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e5\u003c/span\u003e));   \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e//\u003c/span\u003e so c = 5 runs first, then b = c runs, then a = b runs\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch2 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eShort Circuiting\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-short-circuiting\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Short Circuiting\" href=\"#short-circuiting\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn any boolean expression with AND /  OR, most languages will evaluate its sub-expressions from left-to-right. The moment the language finds a condition that satisfies or invalidates the boolean expression, it will skip evaluating the rest of the sub-expressions. This can dramatically speed up your code!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eFor example, this code here:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"if(is_cute() || is_smart() || is_rich()) {\n  date_person();\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e(is_cute() || is_smart() || is_rich()) {\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003edate_person\u003c/span\u003e();\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eReally executes like this:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"if (is_cute()) \n  date_person();\nelse if (is_smart())\n  date_person();\nelse if (is_rich())\n  date_person();\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e (is_cute()) \n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003edate_person\u003c/span\u003e();\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eelse\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e (is_smart())\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003edate_person\u003c/span\u003e();\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eelse\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e (is_rich())\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003edate_person\u003c/span\u003e();\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAnd this code here:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"if (good_salary() \u0026amp;\u0026amp; fun_work() \u0026amp;\u0026amp; good_culture()) {\n  take_job();\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e (good_salary() \u0026amp;\u0026amp; fun_work() \u0026amp;\u0026amp; good_culture()) {\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003etake_job\u003c/span\u003e();\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eReally executes like this:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"if (good_salary()) {\n  if (fun_work()) {\n    if (good_culture()) {\n      take_job();\n    }\n  }\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e (good_salary()) {\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e (\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003efun_work\u003c/span\u003e()) {\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e (\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003egood_culture\u003c/span\u003e()) {\n      \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003etake_job\u003c/span\u003e();\n    }\n  }\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSome languages, like Kotlin, have the ability to use boolean expressions with short circuiting and without!  e.g.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-kotlin notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"fun rich(salary: Int): Boolean {\n  if (salary \u0026gt; 200000) return true;\n  print(\u0026quot;Not in silicon valley!\\n\u0026quot;)\n  return false\n}\n\nfun main() {\n  val cute = true; val smart = false;\n  val salary = 200000;\n    \n  if (cute == true || smart == true || rich(salary) == true)  // uses short circuiting\n    print(\u0026quot;You're my type!\\n\u0026quot;)\n\n  if (cute == true or smart == true or rich(salary) == true)  // no short-circuiting\n    print(\u0026quot;I still want to date you!\\n\u0026quot;)\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003efun\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003erich\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-pds\"\u003e\"\u003c/span\u003eNot in silicon valley!\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003efalse\u003c/span\u003e\n}\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003efun\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003etrue\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eor\u003c/span\u003e smart \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003e==\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003etrue\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eor\u003c/span\u003e rich(salary) \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003e==\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch2 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eIteration\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-iteration\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Iteration\" href=\"#iteration\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThere are three different types of iteration that we'll study:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCounter-controlled: We use a counter, like we would in a for-loop in C++ or using a range in Python:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"    for i in range(1,10):\n        do_something()\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003e    for i in range(1,10):\n        do_something()\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCondition-controlled: We use a boolean condition to decide how to long to loop - this is almost always a while() or do-while() loop.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"    while (condition)\n       do_something()\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003e    while (condition)\n       do_something()\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eCollection-controlled: We're enumerating the items in a collection\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"   for (x in container)\n        do_something(x)\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003e   for (x in container)\n        do_something(x)\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch2 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eIteration in loops\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-iteration-in-loops\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Iteration in loops\" href=\"#iteration-in-loops\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn languages like C++ with simple counter-controlled loops (e.g., for(int i=0;i\u0026lt;10;i++) { ... }) it''s easy to see how looping works. But how does looping work when we use a collection-controlled loop (e.g., iterating thru a vector of objects) or use a range()? There''s more going on under the hood than you think!  There are two primary approaches: using an iterator (of which there are two types of iterators), or using first-class functions.  Let''s learn both!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThese kinds of loops are implemented using \"iterators\":\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"for n in student_names:\n print(n)\n\nfor i in range(0,10):\n  print(i)\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003efor\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003en\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ein\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003estudent_names\u003c/span\u003e:\n \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003en\u003c/span\u003e)\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003efor\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ei\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ein\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003erange\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e0\u003c/span\u003e,\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e10\u003c/span\u003e):\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ei\u003c/span\u003e)\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThese kinds of loops are implemented using first-class functions:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-swift notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"fruits.forEach\n\t{ f -\u0026gt; An iterator provides a way to access the values of a sequence without exposing the sequence's underlying implementation.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIterators can be used to enumerate the values held in a container (e.g., a list, set)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIterators can be used to enumerate the contents of external sources like data files\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIterators can be used to enumerate the values of an abstract sequence. An abstract sequence is one where the values in the sequence aren't stored anywhere, but generated as they're retrieved via the iterator\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIterators used with containers and external sources are typically distinct objects from the objects that contain the sequences they refer to.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch4 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eIterable Objects vs Iterators\u003c/h4\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-iterable-objects-vs-iterators\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Iterable Objects vs Iterators\" href=\"#iterable-objects-vs-iterators\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAn Iterable Object is an object like a container (e.g., vector, list, set) which can have its items iterated over.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAn Iterator is a thing that can refer to an Iterable Object and be used to iterate over the vaulues it holds or represents.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eTo get an iterator, you request one from an iterable object. The Iterable Object then returns the iterator to you. You may then use the iterator to refer to the items in the iterable object.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eExamples of getting an iterator from an iterable object:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv dereferenced.  They're a notable exception to iterators in most languages!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"// C++ has unusual iterator syntax!\nvector\u0026lt;string\u0026gt; iterators:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWith Traditional Classes: An iterator is an object, implemented using regular OOP classes.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\"True Iterators\" aka Generators: An iterator is implemented by using a special type of function called a generator.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eLet's see an iterator that is built using a traditional class - Python makes it easy.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eFirst we must define an iterable class (e.g., like a vector class) which we can iterate over. To make a class iterable, we must define an __iter__() method that creates and returns an iterator object. The ListsOfStrings class is an \u003cem\u003eiterable\u003c/em\u003e class - NOT an iterator.  If its __iter__ method is called, it will return an iterator that can be used to iterate over its items.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"class ListOfStrings:\n  def __init__(self):\n    self.array = []\n\n  def add(self,val):\n    self.array.append(val)\n\n  def __iter__(self):\n    it = OurIterator(self.array)   // calling __iter__ returns a new OurIterator object\n    return it\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eself\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003earray\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eappend\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eval\u003c/span\u003e)\n\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003e__iter__\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e//\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ecalling\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003e__iter__\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ereturns\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ea\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003enew\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-v\"\u003eOurIterator\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eobject\u003c/span\u003e\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003ereturn\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eit\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSecond we can define our OurIterator class. Notice that this iterator class is uniqely tied to our ListOfStrings class above. The two work hand-in-hand.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"class OurIterator:\n  def __init__(self, arr):\n    self.arr = arr\n    self.pos = 0\n\n  def __next__(self):\n    if self.pos \u0026lt; len(self.arr):\n      val = self.arr[self.pos]\n      self.pos += 1\n      return val\n    else:\n      raise StopIteration\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eclass\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-v\"\u003eOurIterator\u003c/span\u003e:\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003e__init__\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eself\u003c/span\u003e, \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eval\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eself\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003earr\u003c/span\u003e[\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eself\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003epos\u003c/span\u003e]\n      \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eself\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003epos\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e+=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e1\u003c/span\u003e\n      \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003ereturn\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eval\u003c/span\u003e\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eelse\u003c/span\u003e:\n      \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eraise\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-v\"\u003eStopIteration\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eOur iterator class must have a method called __next__() that gets the value of the item pointed to by the iterator, advances the iterator, and returns the value. As you can see, if the iterator has run out of items to iterate over (ther are no more items in the iterable object), the iterator (in Python) will throw an exception to tell the user of the iterator that there are no more items left.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIf we define our iterable class and iterator classes this way, Python will give us first-class looping support over our iterable class!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"nerds = ListOfStrings()\nnerds.add(\u0026quot;Carey\u0026quot;)\nnerds.add(\u0026quot;David\u0026quot;)\nnerds.add(\u0026quot;Paul\u0026quot;)\n\nfor n in nerds: \n  print(n)\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003enerds\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-v\"\u003eListOfStrings\u003c/span\u003e()\n\u003cspan note:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe ListOfStrings class implements Java's Iterable interface, which makes this clas an Iterable class (it can have its items iterated over). The class must then implement the iterator() method which can be used to obtain an iterator into the iterable object.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe Iterator class is a nested class within ListOfStrings, which lets it access the memer variables of ListOfStrings.  Notice how it implements the Iterator interface. To do so, it must provide a hasNext() method and a next() method.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAnd here's how we'd use our class.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-java notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"public void testOurList() {         \n  ListOfStrings nerds = \n    new ListOfStrings();\n\n  nerds.add(\u0026quot;Carey\u0026quot;);\n  nerds.add(\u0026quot;David\u0026quot;);\n  nerds.add(\u0026quot;Paul\u0026quot;);\n\n  Iterator it = nerds.iterator(); Or we can use Java's looping syntax, and Java will use syntactic sugar, hiding the calls to get the iterator and use hasNext() and next().\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eOk, next let's see how to create a range() iterable using a class like we have in python (and many other languages):\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"class OurIter:  # iterator class\n  def __init__(self, start, end):\n   self.cur = start\n   self.end = end\n\n  def __next__(self):\n    if self.cur \u0026lt; self.end:\n      val = self.cur\n      self.cur += 1\n      return val\n    else:\n      raise StopIteration()\n\nclass OurRange:  # iterable class\n  def __init__(self, end):\n    self.end = end\n\n  def __iter__(self):\n    iter = OurIter(0, self.end)\n    return iter\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eclass\u003c/span\u003e class=\"pl-c\"\u003e# iterable class\u003c/span\u003e\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003e__init__\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eend\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eend\u003c/span\u003e\n\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003e__iter__\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eself\u003c/span\u003e):\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eiter\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-v\"\u003eOurIter\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e0\u003c/span\u003e, \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eself\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eend\u003c/span\u003e)\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003ereturn\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003eiter\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eNotice the OurRange class, which is an iterable.  We construct it with the maximum value to iterate to, so it will iterate from [0,N).  The class implements an __iter__() method which is called to obtain an iterator into the iterable.  You can see that this method creates a new OurIter iterator, specifying the starting and ending range values.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe OurIter class simply implements a __next__ method, just like any other iterator in python. This method obtains the next value if one is available,  then advances so the next call will return the next value in the range, and finally returns the value. If no more values are available in the range, the method raises a StopIteration exception, which is the way that Python indicates the iterator has reached the end of its iteration.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch4 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\"True Iterators\" aka Generators\u003c/h4\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-true-iterators-aka-generators\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: \u0026quot;True Iterators\u0026quot; aka Generators\" href=\"#true-iterators-aka-generators\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA \"true iterator\" is an iterator that is implemented by using a special type of function called a generator.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA generator is essentially a closure that can be paused and resumed over time. Many languages have generators, but we'll use python to illustrate the concept since the syntax is pretty simple. But first let's start with pseudocode:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"def foo(n):\n  for i in range (1, n):\n    print(f'i is {i}')\n    pause\n    print('woot!')\n\ndef main():\n  p = create_pausable(func=foo, n=4)  # Line A\n  print('CS')\n  start(p)    # Line B\n  print('is')\n  resume(p)  # Line C\n  print('cool!')\n  resume(p)\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003efoo\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003en\u003c/span\u003e):\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003efor\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ei\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ein\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003erange\u003c/span\u003e (\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e1\u003c/span\u003e, \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003en\u003c/span\u003e):\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003ef'i is \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-kos\"\u003e{\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ei\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-kos\"\u003e}\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e'\u003c/span\u003e)\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003epause\u003c/span\u003e\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'woot!'\u003c/span\u003e)\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e():\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003ecreate_pausable\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003efunc\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003efoo\u003c/span\u003e, \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003en\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e4\u003c/span\u003e)  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e# Line A\u003c/span\u003e\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'CS'\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003estart\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e)    \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e# Line B\u003c/span\u003e\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'is'\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eresume\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e)  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e# Line C\u003c/span\u003e\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'cool!'\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eresume\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e)\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHere's the general idea - remember the above is pseudocode:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eLine A: You create a special closure (called a generator), which has one extra piece of data - an instruction pointer. The instruction pointer starts off on the first line of the function, and the function starts in a suspended state. It's not running at this point. We've just created the closure.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eLine B: We wake up the generator/closure begin running it. It will run until it hits the pause command.  At that point, the instruction pointer is saved in the closure so we know where to continue running, and all changes made to the local variables in the closure are also retained. The generator returns to the next line of the main function and keeps running there. Later when we resume the generator, it continues running where it left off, with the same variables/values.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eLine C: We resume the generator, and it continues running where it left off last time\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo the above pseudocode would print:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"CS\ni is 1\nis\nwoot!\ni is 2\ncool!\nwoot!\ni is 3\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eCS\ni is 1\nis\nwoot!\ni is 2\ncool!\nwoot!\ni is 3\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eNow here's the real python syntax:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"def foo(n):\n  for i in range (1, n):\n    print(f'i is {i}')\n    yield\n    print('woot!')\n\ndef main():\n  p = foo(4)\n  print('CS')\n  next(p)\n  print('is')\n  next(p)\n  print('cool!')\n  next(p)\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003efoo\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003en\u003c/span\u003e):\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003efor\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ei\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ein\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003erange\u003c/span\u003e (\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e1\u003c/span\u003e, \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003en\u003c/span\u003e):\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003ef'i is \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eyield\u003c/span\u003e\n    \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'woot!'\u003c/span\u003e)\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e():\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003efoo\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e4\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'CS'\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003enext\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'is'\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003enext\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e'cool!'\u003c/span\u003e)\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003enext\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ep\u003c/span\u003e)\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHere's what changed:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eInstead of \"pause\" we use the keyword \"yield\" to pause the function\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eInsetad of calling create_pausable to create our closure, we just call the function and pass in the arguments, then store the return value in a variable. This does NOT run the function. It just creates the closure, and then returns an object reference to the closure. We then store the object reference to the closure inside our variable p. How does Python know not to run the function but just to create a closure? Because it has a \"yield\" command inside of it. This implicitly tells Python this is a generator function and not a traditional function, and that the first call should not run the function, but just create a closure\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eInstead of calling \"start\" to start the generator running, and \"resume\" to resume its execution, we just use next() on the closure that was returned during the initial call.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBy the way, to use our earler iterable/iterator nomenclature, the generator function is an \"iterable\", and the closure that is returned when it's first called behaves like an iterator.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eNow let's see a more complex generator - it can not only yield its execution, but every time it yields it can return a value to the caller!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"def bar(a, b):\n  while a \u0026gt; b:\n    yield a  # Line A\n    a -= 1\n\nf = bar(3, 0)\nprint('Eat prunes!')\nt = next(f)  # Line B\nprint(t)\nt = next(f)\nprint(t)\nt = next(f)\nprint(t)\nprint(f'Explosive diarrhea!')\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003edef\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e# Line B\u003c/span\u003e\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003et\u003c/span\u003e)\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003et\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003enext\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ef\u003c/span\u003e)\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003et\u003c/span\u003e)\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003et\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003enext\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ef\u003c/span\u003e)\n\u003cspan So not only does it pause itself, but it also returns a value at this time. Notice that on Line B, we wake up the generator and let it run until it yields a value. The yielded value is returned by next() and that value is stored in the t variable.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThis is a more idiomatic use of a generator – they're used to  \"generate\" a sequence of values that can be retrieved one at a time – the sequence might be finite, or infinite!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSince a generator is an iterable object, and produces an iterator... \u003cspan class=\"pl-smi\"\u003econsole\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-kos\"\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003elog\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan Kotlin:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-kotlin notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"// Kotlin generators care called \u0026quot;sequences\u0026quot;\nfun vals(start: Int) = sequence {\n  var i = start\n  while (true) {\n    yield(i)\n    ++i\n  }\n}\n\nfun main(args: Array\u0026lt;String\u0026gt;) {\n  val iter = vals(42).iterator()\n  var count = 3\n  while (count-- \u0026gt; 0) {\n    var n = iter.next()\n    println(n)\n  } \n  \n  for (n in vals(7).take(5))  \n    println(n)     // 7, 8, 9, 10, 11\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e//\u003c/span\u003e Kotlin generators care called \"sequences\"\u003c/span\u003e\n\u003cspan Class-based Iterators and Generators\u003c/h4\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-analysis-of-class-based-iterators-and-generators\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Analysis of Class-based aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 generators. But the two are isomorphic and functionally equivalent - anything we can do with a class we can do with a generator, and visa-versa.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eFirst-class function-based iteration\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-first-class-function-based-iteration\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: First-class function-based iteration\" href=\"#first-class-function-based-iteration\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAlright, we'll end this lecture by looking at how some languages use first-class functions to do iteration. The syntax often looks something like this:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"var fruits = ... // some list of fruits\n\nfruits.forEach { f -\u0026gt; println(\u0026quot;$f\u0026quot;) }\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003evar fruits = ... // some list of fruits\n\nfruits.forEach { f -\u0026gt; println(\"$f\") }\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBasically, the container (e.g., vector, list or set) contains a forEach() method, which accepts one parameter.  We pass in a { lambda } function to the forEeach method as this parameter.  The forEach method iterates  over each item in the iterable, and passes each item as the parameter to the lambda function (e.g., to the parameter f shown in the lambda above).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHere are some examples:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-rust notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" we're NOT using an iterator! Instead, we're passing a function as an argument to a forEach()/each() method that loops over the iterable's items!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eLet's build a forEach() method for our earlier Python container:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"class ListOfStrings:\n  def __init__(self):\n    self.array = []\n\n  def add(self,val):\n    self.array.append(val)\n\n  def forEach(self, f):   # define our own for-each method\n    for x in self.array:\n      f(x)\n\nyummy = ListOfStrings()\n... # add items to yummy\n\ndef like(x):\n print(f'I like {x}'))\n\nyummy.forEach(like)\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eforEach\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003elike\u003c/span\u003e)\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eNotice how the iterable object (a <div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h1 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Controlla-palooza</h1><a id="user-content-controlla-palooza" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Controlla-palooza" href="#controlla-palooza"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Everything you never learned about how code executes!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Expressions</h2><a id="user-content-expressions" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Expressions" href="#expressions"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">An expression is a combination of values, function calls and operations that, when evaluated, produces an output value. An expression may or may not have side effects.</p>
<p dir="auto">Which of the following are expressions?</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>f(x)*5
-- Yes!</li>
<li>if (foo)
cout &lt;&lt; "bar";
-- No</li>
<li>a = 5
-- In some languages, yes. Like in C++ we can do b = a = 5, so a = 5 is an expression that has a side effect and produces a result</li>
<li>a &amp;&amp; (b || c)
-- Yes!</li>
<li>if a &gt; 5 then a<em>3 else a</em>4
-- Yes - in functional languages (or languages with functional features) this if code is an expression, which produces a result of either a<em>3 or a</em>4</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Expression Evaluation Order</h2><a id="user-content-expression-evaluation-order" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Expression Evaluation Order" href="#expression-evaluation-order"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Consider the following C++ code:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="int c = 1, d = 2;

int f() {
  ++c;
  return 0;
}

int main() {
  cout &lt;&lt; c - d + f() ;
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">int</span> c = <span class="pl-c1">1</span>, d = <span class="pl-c1">2</span>;

<span class="pl-k">int</span> <span class="pl-en">f</span>() {
  ++c;
  <span class="pl-k">return</span> <span class="pl-c1">0</span>;
}

<span class="pl-k">int</span> <span class="pl-en">main</span>() {
  cout &lt;&lt; c - d + <span class="pl-c1">f</span>() ;
}</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Or this code:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="int c = 0; 

int f() {
  ++c;
  return c;
}

void h(int a, int b)
  { cout &lt;&lt; a &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot;&lt;&lt; b; }

int main() {
  h(c, f());
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">int</span> c = <span class="pl-c1">0</span>; 

<span class="pl-k">int</span> <span class="pl-en">f</span>() {
  ++c;
  <span class="pl-k">return</span> c;
}

<span class="pl-k">void</span> <span class="pl-en">h</span>(<span class="pl-k">int</span> a, <span class="pl-k">int</span> b)
  { cout &lt;&lt; a &lt;&lt; <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span> <span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>&lt;&lt; b; }

<span class="pl-k">int</span> <span class="pl-en">main</span>() {
  <span class="pl-c1">h</span>(c, <span class="pl-c1">f</span>());
}</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Challenge: What will these C++ programs print?<br>
The first program produces either -1 or 0
The second program produces either 0 1 or 1 1</p>
<p dir="auto">Why? C++ doesn't specify what order the terms of an arithmetic expression must be evaluated! Nor does it specify what order parameters must be evaluated! Some languages mandate a left-to-right eval order (C#, Java, JavaScript, Swift), while others don't (C++, Rust, OCaml).</p>
<p dir="auto">So in general, if your expressions are calling functions, those functions shouldn't have side effects that could impact the execution of the other functions called in the expression. Otherwise you'll get unpredictable behavior depending on the compiler and potentially each time you compile the code it could do a different thing!</p>
<p dir="auto">Why avoid specifiying ane explicit evaluation order? The reason that an ordering is not specified is to give the compiler latitude to optimize the code.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Associativity</h2><a id="user-content-associativity" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Associativity" href="#associativity"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Regardless what order each of the terms are evaluated... Most languages use left-to-right associativity when evaluating mathematical expressions. Which is the way we all learned to do it in school.</p>
<p dir="auto">So an expression like:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="cout &lt;&lt; c - d + f() ;"><pre>cout &lt;&lt; c - d + f() ;</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Would be evaluated as:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="cout &lt;&lt; (c - d) + f() ;"><pre>cout &lt;&lt; (c - d) + f() ;</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Instead of:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="cout &lt;&lt; c - (d + f());"><pre>cout &lt;&lt; c - (d + f());</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">There are some notable exceptions, like evaluating exponentiation (e.g., 2^3^2 == 512, since we often evaluate exponentiation from right-to-left).  Assignment is also right-associative, so we can do something like this:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="  a = b = c = 5;  
  // equivalent to:
  a  = (b = (c = 5));   // so c = 5 runs first, then b = c runs, then a = b runs"><pre>  a = b = c = <span class="pl-c1">5</span>;  
  <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> equivalent to:</span>
  a  = (b = (c = <span class="pl-c1">5</span>));   <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> so c = 5 runs first, then b = c runs, then a = b runs</span></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Short Circuiting</h2><a id="user-content-short-circuiting" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Short Circuiting" href="#short-circuiting"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">In any boolean expression with AND /  OR, most languages will evaluate its sub-expressions from left-to-right. The moment the language finds a condition that satisfies or invalidates the boolean expression, it will skip evaluating the rest of the sub-expressions. This can dramatically speed up your code!</p>
<p dir="auto">For example, this code here:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="if(is_cute() || is_smart() || is_rich()) {
  date_person();
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">if</span>(is_cute() || is_smart() || is_rich()) {
  <span class="pl-c1">date_person</span>();
}</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Really executes like this:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="if (is_cute()) 
  date_person();
else if (is_smart())
  date_person();
else if (is_rich())
  date_person();"><pre><span class="pl-k">if</span> (is_cute()) 
  <span class="pl-en">date_person</span>();
<span class="pl-k">else</span> <span class="pl-k">if</span> (is_smart())
  <span class="pl-en">date_person</span>();
<span class="pl-k">else</span> <span class="pl-k">if</span> (is_rich())
  <span class="pl-en">date_person</span>();</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">And this code here:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="if (good_salary() &amp;&amp; fun_work() &amp;&amp; good_culture()) {
  take_job();
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">if</span> (good_salary() &amp;&amp; fun_work() &amp;&amp; good_culture()) {
  <span class="pl-c1">take_job</span>();
}</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Really executes like this:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="if (good_salary()) {
  if (fun_work()) {
    if (good_culture()) {
      take_job();
    }
  }
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">if</span> (good_salary()) {
  <span class="pl-k">if</span> (<span class="pl-c1">fun_work</span>()) {
    <span class="pl-k">if</span> (<span class="pl-c1">good_culture</span>()) {
      <span class="pl-c1">take_job</span>();
    }
  }
}</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Some languages, like Kotlin, have the ability to use boolean expressions with short circuiting and without!  e.g.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-kotlin notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="fun rich(salary: Int): Boolean {
  if (salary &gt; 200000) return true;
  print(&quot;Not in silicon valley!\n&quot;)
  return false
}

fun main() {
  val cute = true; val smart = false;
  val salary = 200000;
    
  if (cute == true || smart == true || rich(salary) == true)  // uses short circuiting
    print(&quot;You're my type!\n&quot;)

  if (cute == true or smart == true or rich(salary) == true)  // no short-circuiting
    print(&quot;I still want to date you!\n&quot;)
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">fun</span> <span class="pl-en">rich</span>(<span class="pl-smi">salary</span><span class="pl-k">:</span> <span class="pl-c1">Int</span>): <span class="pl-c1">Boolean</span> {
  <span class="pl-k">if</span> (salary <span class="pl-k">&gt;</span> <span class="pl-c1">200000</span>) <span class="pl-k">return</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span>;
  <span class="pl-c1">print</span>(<span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>Not in silicon valley!<span class="pl-cce">\n</span><span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>)
  <span class="pl-k">return</span> <span class="pl-c1">false</span>
}

<span class="pl-k">fun</span> <span class="pl-en">main</span>() {
  <span class="pl-k">val</span> cute <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span>; <span class="pl-k">val</span> smart <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">false</span>;
  <span class="pl-k">val</span> salary <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">200000</span>;
    
  <span class="pl-k">if</span> (cute <span class="pl-k">==</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span> <span class="pl-k">||</span> smart <span class="pl-k">==</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span> <span class="pl-k">||</span> rich(salary) <span class="pl-k">==</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span>)  <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> uses short circuiting</span>
    <span class="pl-c1">print</span>(<span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>You're my type!<span class="pl-cce">\n</span><span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>)

  <span class="pl-k">if</span> (cute <span class="pl-k">==</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span> <span class="pl-k">or</span> smart <span class="pl-k">==</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span> <span class="pl-k">or</span> rich(salary) <span class="pl-k">==</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span>)  <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> no short-circuiting</span>
    <span class="pl-c1">print</span>(<span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>I still want to date you!<span class="pl-cce">\n</span><span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>)
}</pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Iteration</h2><a id="user-content-iteration" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Iteration" href="#iteration"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">There are three different types of iteration that we'll study:</p>
<p dir="auto">Counter-controlled: We use a counter, like we would in a for-loop in C++ or using a range in Python:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="    for i in range(1,10):
        do_something()"><pre class="notranslate"><code>    for i in range(1,10):
        do_something()
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Condition-controlled: We use a boolean condition to decide how to long to loop - this is almost always a while() or do-while() loop.</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="    while (condition)
       do_something()"><pre class="notranslate"><code>    while (condition)
       do_something()
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Collection-controlled: We're enumerating the items in a collection</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="   for (x in container)
        do_something(x)"><pre class="notranslate"><code>   for (x in container)
        do_something(x)
</code></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Iteration in loops</h2><a id="user-content-iteration-in-loops" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Iteration in loops" href="#iteration-in-loops"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">In languages like C++ with simple counter-controlled loops (e.g., for(int i=0;i&lt;10;i++) { ... }) it''s easy to see how looping works. But how does looping work when we use a collection-controlled loop (e.g., iterating thru a vector of objects) or use a range()? There''s more going on under the hood than you think!  There are two primary approaches: using an iterator (of which there are two types of iterators), or using first-class functions.  Let''s learn both!</p>
<p dir="auto">These kinds of loops are implemented using "iterators":</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="for n in student_names:
 print(n)

for i in range(0,10):
  print(i)"><pre><span class="pl-k">for</span> <span class="pl-s1">n</span> <span class="pl-c1">in</span> <span class="pl-s1">student_names</span>:
 <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s1">n</span>)

<span class="pl-k">for</span> <span class="pl-s1">i</span> <span class="pl-c1">in</span> <span class="pl-en">range</span>(<span class="pl-c1">0</span>,<span class="pl-c1">10</span>):
  <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s1">i</span>)</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">These kinds of loops are implemented using first-class functions:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-swift notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="fruits.forEach
	{ f -&gt; println(&quot;$f&quot;) }"><pre>fruits<span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-en">forEach</span>
	<span class="pl-kos">{</span> f <span class="pl-c1">-&gt;</span> <span class="pl-smi">println</span><span class="pl-k"></span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-s">"</span><span class="pl-s">$f</span><span class="pl-s">"</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span> <span class="pl-kos">}</span></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Iterator-based loops</h3><a id="user-content-iterator-based-loops" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Iterator-based loops" href="#iterator-based-loops"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">An iterator is an object that enables enumeration of a sequence of values. An iterator provides a way to access the values of a sequence without exposing the sequence's underlying implementation.</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Iterators can be used to enumerate the values held in a container (e.g., a list, set)</li>
<li>Iterators can be used to enumerate the contents of external sources like data files</li>
<li>Iterators can be used to enumerate the values of an abstract sequence. An abstract sequence is one where the values in the sequence aren't stored anywhere, but generated as they're retrieved via the iterator</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Iterators used with containers and external sources are typically distinct objects from the objects that contain the sequences they refer to.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Iterable Objects vs Iterators</h4><a id="user-content-iterable-objects-vs-iterators" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Iterable Objects vs Iterators" href="#iterable-objects-vs-iterators"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>
<p dir="auto">An Iterable Object is an object like a container (e.g., vector, list, set) which can have its items iterated over.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p dir="auto">An Iterator is a thing that can refer to an Iterable Object and be used to iterate over the vaulues it holds or represents.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">To get an iterator, you request one from an iterable object. The Iterable Object then returns the iterator to you. You may then use the iterator to refer to the items in the iterable object.</p>
<p dir="auto">Examples of getting an iterator from an iterable object:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-kotlin notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="// Kotlin: iterator into container
val numbers = listOf(10,20,30,42)

val it = numbers.iterator()  // get an iterator into the numbers list"><pre><span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> Kotlin: iterator into container</span>
<span class="pl-k">val</span> numbers <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">listOf</span>(<span class="pl-c1">10</span>,<span class="pl-c1">20</span>,<span class="pl-c1">30</span>,<span class="pl-c1">42</span>)

<span class="pl-k">val</span> it <span class="pl-k">=</span> numbers.iterator()  <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> get an iterator into the numbers list</span></pre></div>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-swift notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="// Swift: abstract sequence
var range = 1...1000000

var it = range.makeIterator()  // get an iterator to the first # of the abstract seq."><pre><span class="pl-c">// Swift: abstract sequence</span>
<span class="pl-k">var</span> <span class="pl-s1">range</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">1</span><span class="pl-c1">...</span><span class="pl-c1">1000000</span>

<span class="pl-k">var</span> <span class="pl-s1">it</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> range<span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-en">makeIterator</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span>  <span class="pl-c">// get an iterator to the first # of the abstract seq.</span></pre></div>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="// C++
vector&lt;string&gt; fruits = {&quot;apple&quot;, &quot;pear&quot;, ... };

auto it = fruits.begin();  // get an iterator into a C++ vector"><pre><span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> C++</span>
vector&lt;string&gt; fruits = {<span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>apple<span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>, <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>pear<span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>, ... };

<span class="pl-k">auto</span> it = fruits.begin();  <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> get an iterator into a C++ vector</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">While iterators differ by language, most* tend to have an interface that look like one of these (* C++ has a notably different approach):</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h5 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">An interface with hasNext() and next() methods</h5><a id="user-content-an-interface-with-hasnext-and-next-methods" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: An interface with hasNext() and next() methods" href="#an-interface-with-hasnext-and-next-methods"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">These types of iterators are used with containers and external sources like data files. They have two methods:</p>
<p dir="auto">iter.hasNext(): We ask the iterator (NOT the iterable object) whether it refers to a valid value that can be retrieved?</p>
<p dir="auto">iter.next(): We ask the iterator (NOT the iterable object) to get the value that it points to, and advance to the next item</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-kotlin notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="// Kotlin: iterator into container
val numbers = listOf(10,20,30,42)

val it = numbers.iterator()
while (it.hasNext()) {
  val v = it.next();
  println(v)
}"><pre><span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> Kotlin: iterator into container</span>
<span class="pl-k">val</span> numbers <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">listOf</span>(<span class="pl-c1">10</span>,<span class="pl-c1">20</span>,<span class="pl-c1">30</span>,<span class="pl-c1">42</span>)

<span class="pl-k">val</span> it <span class="pl-k">=</span> numbers.iterator()
<span class="pl-k">while</span> (it.hasNext()) {
  <span class="pl-k">val</span> v <span class="pl-k">=</span> it.next();
  <span class="pl-c1">println</span>(v)
}</pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h5 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">An interface with just next() methods</h5><a id="user-content-an-interface-with-just-next-methods" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: An interface with just next() methods" href="#an-interface-with-just-next-methods"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">These types of iterators are used with Abstract Sequences, like ranges: range(1,10)</p>
<p dir="auto">iter.next(): The iterator (NOT the iterable object) generates and returns the next value in the sequence, or indicates the sequence is over via a return code or by throwing an exception</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-swift notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="// Swift: abstract sequence
var range = 1...1000000

var it = range.makeIterator()
while true {
  var v = it.next()  // in Swift, the next() method returns either nil or a valid value
  if (v == nil) { break }
  print(v)
}"><pre><span class="pl-c">// Swift: abstract sequence</span>
<span class="pl-k">var</span> <span class="pl-s1">range</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">1</span><span class="pl-c1">...</span><span class="pl-c1">1000000</span>

<span class="pl-k">var</span> <span class="pl-s1">it</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> range<span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-en">makeIterator</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span>
while true <span class="pl-kos">{</span>
  <span class="pl-k">var</span> <span class="pl-s1">v</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> it<span class="pl-kos">.</span><span class="pl-en">next</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span>  <span class="pl-c">// in Swift, the next() method returns either nil or a valid value</span>
  if <span class="pl-kos">(</span>v <span class="pl-c1">==</span> <span class="pl-smi">nil</span><span class="pl-kos">)</span> <span class="pl-kos">{</span> break <span class="pl-kos">}</span>
  <span class="pl-en">print</span><span class="pl-kos">(</span>v<span class="pl-kos">)</span>
<span class="pl-kos">}</span></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h4 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">C++ has unique iterators</h4><a id="user-content-c-has-unique-iterators" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: C++ has unique iterators" href="#c-has-unique-iterators"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">C++ models its iterators after C++ pointers, which can be incremented, decremented, and dereferenced.  They're a notable exception to iterators in most languages!</p>
